Joint Liability for Diyah: Two Cutters, One Hand
Explanation of the matn phrase 'وضمنا ديتها' with intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two persons cut off one hand and explain the apportionment of diyah.

Core Idea
Two cutters jointly guarantee full diyah for one hand; each owes half (quarter of total).

This lesson explains the matn statement 'وضمنا ديتها' — we guarantee its blood-money. The author addresses the scenario where two individuals cut off a single hand. The ruling clarifies joint liability, the amount each must pay, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and matn phrase
Locate the phrase 'وضمنا ديتها' and note that it refers to the guarantee of blood-money by the two cutters. The context is amputation of a hand by two persons.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq; the sharh expands on joint liability.
2
Understand the joint guarantee and apportionment
The author states that because the destruction happened by both their acts, each is liable for half the diyah of a hand (i.e., quarter of full diyah each). The payment is from their own wealth, not the 'aqila, because the act is intentional.
Full diyah of a hand is 1/2 of full diyah (100 camels for a life). So each pays 1/4 of full diyah (25 camels equivalent).
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf initially held that only the diyah of the body (i.e., the full diyah of the hand) is due, not apportioned? Actually the disagreement is about the basis: Abu Yusuf said the diyah of the body (1/2 diyah) is due, but the correct view (Abu Hanifa) is that full diyah (as if the hand is worth full? Wait: the text says 'يجب دية البد لا غير' meaning only the diyah of the body is due? Actually it says 'يجب دية البد لا غير' which is ambiguous. The correct interpretation: Abu Yusuf held that only the diyah of the body (i.e., the diyah for the hand) is due, not additional? Actually the matn says 'وضمنا ديتها' meaning they guarantee its diyah (the hand's diyah). Abu Yusuf's view might be that only the diyah of the body (the whole hand) is due, meaning they don't pay half each but something else? The text says 'والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة' so Abu Hanifa's view is that full diyah is required, each paying half. That is correct.
The dispute is over the basis of calculation, but the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات باب القصاص فيما دون النفس وضمنا ديتها وأن قطع واحد يميني رجلين فلهما قطع يمينه ونصف الدية وإن حضر كف الفخد اليد وحكومة العدل فيما وراء الكف. وعن أبي يوسف ومن تابعه في المسألة الأولى أنه يجب دية البد لا غير والصحيح قول أبي حنيفة وفي الظهيرية ولو قطع رجل ثلاثة أصابع من رجل خطأ ثم قطع آخر أصبعين ثم شلت الكف من الجراحتين فعلى الأول دية ما قطع. وعلى الثاني دية ما قطع ما بقي من الكف بعد الأصابع فهو نصفان؛ فما يصيب صاحب الأكثر دخل أرش الأقل في الأكثر، وأما النصف الآخر إن كان الآخر قطع أصبعين فعليه خمسا دية الأصل وهو عشر الدية، وفي الأنملة حكومة عدل والظفر إذا نبت كما كان لا شيء فيه، وإن نبت على عيب فحكومة دون الأولى. وفي الينابيع: إذا قطع اليد من العضد والرجل من فعندهما فيه الدية وما فوق الكف والقدم ففيه حكومة عدل، وعند أبي يوسف ما فوق الكعب إلى القدم تبع للأصابع. وإذا كسر يد عبد رجل أو رجل لا يجب في الحال شيء. وفي الكافي: ولو قطع اليد وفيها ثلاث أصابع فعليه ثلاثة أخماس دية اليد ولا شيء في الكف بالإجماع وقاطع يد لا كف له فلا قصاص عليه في الساعد. وقال أبو يوسف: إذا كانا سواء اقتص منه وعلى هذا الاختلاف إذا قطع كف رجل وفيها أصبع زائدة وفي يد القاطع أصبع زائدة، ولو قطع أصبعاً زائداً في يده مثلها لا قصاص بالإجماع. وقال أبو حنيفة في الأقطعين والأشلين إنه لا قصاص وهو قول أبي يوسف في رواية الحسن عنه، وذلك مقطوع الإبهام والأصبع كلها إذا قطع يد أشل فلا قصاص في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وفي الخانية: ولو قطع أظافر اليدين أو الرجلين روى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا قصاص فيه وفيه حكومة عدل، ولو كسر عظماً من ساعد أو ساق أو ترقوة أو غيره ففيه حكومة عدل. قال رحمه الله: (وضمنا ديتها) أي ضمن القاطعان دية المقطوع لأن التلف حصل بفعلهما فيجب عليهما نصف الدية على كل واحد منهما الربع فتجب في مالهما لأن العاقلة لا تتحمل العمد.
Book of Injuries, Chapter of Retaliation for Less Than Life. And we guarantee its blood-money, and that one who cuts the right hands of two men, then they have cutting of his right hand and half the blood-money. And if one comes, the palm, the thigh, the hand, and the arbitration of just value for what is beyond the palm. And from Abu Yusuf and those who followed him in the first issue that the blood-money of the body is due only, and the correct is the statement of Abu Hanifa. And in al-Zuhayriyya: If a man cuts three fingers of a man by mistake, then another cuts two fingers, then the palm becomes paralyzed from the two wounds, then on the first is the blood-money of what he cut. And on the second is the blood-money of what he cut; what remains of the palm after the fingers is half; so what falls to the owner of the greater enters the arsh of the lesser into the greater. And as for the other half, if the other cut two fingers, then on him is two-fifths of the original blood-money, which is one-tenth of the full blood-money. And for the finger-bone segment is arbitration of just value. And the nail if it grows as it was, nothing is due; if it grows defective, then arbitration less than the first. And in al-Yanabi': If the hand is cut from the upper arm and the leg from its joint, then in them is the full blood-money, and for what is above the palm and foot is arbitration of just value. And according to Abu Yusuf, what is above the ankle to the foot is a follower of the fingers. And if one breaks the hand of a slave of a man or a leg, nothing is due immediately. And in al-Kafi: If one cuts a hand and it has three fingers, then on him is three-fifths of the blood-money of the hand, and nothing for the palm by consensus. And a cutter of a hand that has no palm has no retaliation on the forearm. And Abu Yusuf said: If they are equal, retaliation is taken from him. And on this difference, if he cuts the palm of a man and it has an extra finger, and in the cutter's hand is an extra finger, and if he cuts an extra finger similar to it, no retaliation by consensus. And Abu Hanifa said regarding the one with missing limb and the paralyzed: there is no retaliation, and it is the statement of Abu Yusuf in the narration of al-Hasan from him. And that is the one whose thumb and all fingers are cut; if he cuts a paralyzed hand, no retaliation in the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And in al-Khaniyya: If he cuts the fingernails of the hands or feet, al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that there is no retaliation and there is arbitration of just value. And if he breaks a bone of the forearm, shin, collarbone, or other, then there is arbitration of just value. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And we guarantee its blood-money), meaning the two cutters guarantee the blood-money of the cut part because the destruction occurred by their action, so half the blood-money is due from them, a quarter from each, so it is due from their wealth because the 'aqila does not bear intentional killing.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on multiple scenarios involving cutting of limbs and fingers, expanding the matn phrase 'وضمنا ديتها' (we guarantee its blood-money). The author begins by presenting the general principle: when two persons cut off a hand, they are jointly liable for full diyah, each paying half (quarter each) from their own wealth because the 'aqila does not bear intentional acts. Then he cites various additional rulings from different Hanafi sources (al-Zuhayriyya, al-Yanabi', al-Kafi, al-Khaniyya) covering cases of mistake, partial cuts, extra fingers, paralyzed limbs, nails, and bones. The correct position is Abu Hanifa's view that full diyah is due, not just the diyah of the body as some held. Detailed calculations are given for finger counts and sequencing of cuts. The passage also clarifies that for nails, if they grow back perfectly, no compensation; if they grow back defective, just value is assessed. For fractures, just value is also assessed. The overall theme is the comprehensive treatment of injuries short of life, demonstrating the Hanafi school's attention to minute details.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood-money; monetary compensation for bodily injury or death, paid by the offender or his 'aqila.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Arbitration of just value; compensation assessed by a just expert for injuries without fixed arsh, based on proportion of loss of value.
أرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for specific injuries (e.g., fingers); also used as a general term for injury compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Each pays full diyah of a hand (half of full diyah).
B) Each pays half of the diyah of a hand (quarter of full diyah).
C) The first cutter pays full diyah, the second pays nothing.
D) They pay one diyah divided equally, each paying full diyah of a hand.

Key Takeaway
When two persons cut one hand, each is liable for half the diyah of that hand (quarter of full diyah) from his own wealth.

Multiple Victims of Limb Cutting: Joint Retaliation
Inter-madhhab dispute on multiple victim retaliation for limb cutting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint retaliation for two victims of the same limb injury and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding sequence.

Core Idea
Equality in the cause of injury gives equal right to retaliation, regardless of sequence.

When one person cuts the right hands of two men, does the sequence of cuts affect the victims' right to retaliate? The Hanafi school holds that both victims share an equal right, while Imam al-Shafi'i distinguishes based on order. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and identifies the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
From the matn and commentary, state clearly what the Hanafi school rules when one person cuts the right hands of two men, assuming both victims come together.
The ruling applies whether the cuts were simultaneous or successive.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that if the cuts were successive, only the first victim may retaliate; the second gets compensation. Understand the basis for this distinction.
The Shafi'i proof relies on the idea that the first cut exhausts the possibility of retaliation for the same limb.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (qiyas)
The Hanafi school uses analogical reasoning: comparing two victims to two creditors in a company, where each has an equal claim on the debtor's assets. Also the analogy of a jointly owned slave.
The key principle is that equality in the cause of entitlement (sabab al-istihqaq) leads to equality in the right.
4
Distinguish the case of pledge (rahn)
Understand why a pledge is treated differently: pledge involves legal seizure where priority matters, unlike bodily injury where the cause is simultaneously established.
This distinction refutes a potential objection that the first victim's right occupies the hand.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
State the outcome: Hanafi allows both to cut the hand together and share half the diyah; Shafi'i gives retaliation only to the first and compensation to the second.
This ikhtilaf affects how a qadi rules when multiple victims of the same injury appear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وأن قطع واحد يميني رجلين فلهما قطع يمينه ونصف الدية) يعني إذا حضرا معاً سواء كان القطع جملة واحدة أو على التعاقب وقال الشافعي: إن قطعهما على التعاقب يقطع للأول منهما ويغرم أرش اليد للثاني. ولنا أن المساواة في سبب الاستحقاق يوجب المساواة في الاستحقاق ولا عبرة في التقدم والتأخر كالغريمين في الشركة. وهذا لأن حق كل واحد منهما ثابت في كل اليد لتقرر السبب في حق كل واحد منهما وهو القطع. وكونه مشغولاً يحق الأول لا يمنع تقرر السبب في حق الثاني، ولهذا لو كان القاطع لهما عبداً استويا في استحقاق رقبته، ولو كان يمنع بالأول لما شاركه الثاني بخلاف الرهن لأنه استيفاء حكماً فلا يثبت للثاني بعد ما ثبت للأول كالاستيفاء حقيقة، فإذا لم يمنع الأول بثبوت حق الثاني فيها استويا فيها يقطع لهما إذا حضرا معاً لعدم الأولوية ويقضى لهما بنصف الدية يقسمانه نصفين لاستوائهما فيه بخلاف ما إذا كان القصاص في النفس حيث يكتفي فيه
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And that one cuts the right hands of two men, then they have cutting his right hand and half the blood-money), meaning if they both come together, whether the cut is at once or successively. And al-Shafi'i said: If he cuts them successively, then the first one cuts his hand, and he pays the arsh of the hand to the second. And we have the argument that equality in the cause of entitlement necessitates equality in entitlement, and priority is not considered, like two creditors in a company. And this is because the right of each is established in the whole hand due to the existence of the cause for each, which is the cut. And the fact that the hand is occupied with the right of the first does not prevent the existence of the cause for the second. Hence if the cutter was a slave for both, they are equal in entitlement to his neck. And if the first would prevent, then the second would not share, unlike a pledge because it is a legal seizure, so the second is not established after the first, as in actual seizure. When the first does not prevent due to the existence of the right of the second in it, they are equal in it; so they both cut it if they come together due to lack of priority, and half the blood-money is decreed for them, dividing it in half due to their equality in it, unlike when the retaliation is for life, where one suffices.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is a ruling-with-proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states: if one person cuts the right hands of two men, both victims have the right to cut the cutter's right hand and also receive half diyah. The commentator explains that this applies when both victims come together, whether the cut was done simultaneously or sequentially. Al-Shafi'i held that if done sequentially, only the first victim can retaliate, and the second gets monetary compensation. The Hanafi response argues that equality in the cause (both sustained the same injury from the same cutter) gives equal rights to retaliation, just as two creditors share in a debtor's assets. The right of each is established in the entire hand; one does not exclude the other. The analogy of a slave: if the cutter was a slave for both, they share in his value. The case of a pledge is distinguished because it involves legal seizure where priority matters. Since neither has priority, if both come together, they jointly cut the hand, and each gets half of the half-diyah (i.e., quarter each). This differs from qisas for life, where only one execution suffices.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation; inflicting a similar injury on the perpetrator as was inflicted on the victim.
سبب الاستحقاق (sabab al-istihqaq): Cause of entitlement; here it is the act of cutting which establishes the victim's right to retaliation.
أرش (arsh): Monetary compensation for injury when retaliation is not possible or waived; here Shafi'i gives it to the second victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man cuts the right hand of two men at different times, what is the ruling when both victims come together to claim retaliation?
A) Only the first victim can cut the perpetrator's hand.
B) Both victims can cut the perpetrator's hand together.
C) Neither can retaliate; both must accept blood-money.
D) The second victim has no right.

Key Takeaway
According to the Hanafi school, multiple victims of the same limb injury from the same perpetrator have equal right to retaliation regardless of the order of cuts.

When Only One Victim Comes to Retaliate
Ruling with proof of present victim's right and analogy to shuf'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that permits a present victim to execute qisas when only one of two victims appears, and explain the analogy to shuf'a.

Core Idea
One present victim may execute qisas; the absent victim gets only half diyah — like pre-emption.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario in qisas: two victims have their hands cut by one person, but only one victim appears at the time of retaliation. The author explains why the present victim can proceed and what the absent victim is entitled to, using an analogy to pre-emption (shuf'a). Understanding this ruling clarifies the interplay between certain and uncertain rights in retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Two victims each lost a hand to one cutter. Only one victim comes to retaliate. The ruling: the present victim may cut the cutter's hand, and the absent victim is entitled to half the diyah from the cutter.
2
Understand the reasoning: certainty vs uncertainty
The present victim's right is certain—he is present to demand qisas. The absent victim's right is uncertain: he might not claim, might forgive, or might settle. Therefore, delaying is not required. The certain right takes precedence.
Hanafi principle: a certain right is not delayed for an uncertain one.
3
Examine the analogy to shuf'a
Like one co-preemptor (shafi') may take the entire property when the other is absent, the present victim may take the full retaliation. The shared illa (effective cause) is the certainty of one right and uncertainty of the other.
This is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) between shuf'a and qisas in this procedural aspect.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on pardon after decree
If retaliation was decreed for both and then one victim pardons before execution, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the other may still execute qisas (qawad), because pardon before execution is like pardon before decree—execution is a punishment, not a shared right. Muhammad says only arsh (compensation) remains because the decree established a shared right.
Mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: qawad remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن حضر واحد فقطع يده له فللآخر عليه نصف الدية) لأن للحاضر أن يستوفي حقه ولا يجب عليه التأخير حتى يحضر الآخر ثبوت حقه بيقين وحق الآخر متردد لاحتمال أن لا يطلب أو يعفو مجاناً أو صلحاً فصار كأحد الشفيعين إذا حضر والآخر غائب حيث يقضى له بالشفعة في الكل لما قلنا . ثم إذا حضر الآخر بعد ما قطعت للآخر وطلب يقضى له بالدية لأن يده وفاءها حق مستحق عليه فيضمنها لسلامتها له. ولو قضى بالقصاص بينهما ثم عفا أحدهما قبل استيفاء الدية فللآخر القود عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وعند محمد له الأرش لأن القصاص بالقضاء أثبت الشركة بينهما فعاد حق كل واحد منهما إلى البعض، فإذا عفا أحدهما فقد منع الآخر من استيفاء الكل. ولهما أن الإمضاء من في العقوبات فالعفو قبله كالعفو قبل القضاء، ولو قطع أحدهما يد القاطع من المرفق سقط القصاص لذهاب اليد التي فيها القصاص بالقطع ظلماً ولا ينقلب مالكاً كما إذا قطعها أجنبي أو سقطت بآفة سماوية، ولهما نصف الدية على حالها لأنها واجبة قبل قطعها ولا تسقط بالقطع ثم القاطع الأول بالخيار إن شاء قطع ذراع القاطع، وإن شاء ضمنه دية اليد وحكومة عدل في قطع الذراع إلى المرفق لأن يد القاطع كانت مقطوعة . الكف من حين قطع القاطع الأول من المرفق فكانت كالشلاء، وعلى هذا لو كان المقطوع يده واحداً فقطع القاطع من المرفق سقط حقه في القصاص ووجب عليه القصاص وللمقطوع من المرفق الخيار إن شاء قطع من المرفق، وإن شاء أخذ الأرش لما ذكرنا وقدمنا له مزيد بيان.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And if one comes and cuts his hand, then the other has half the blood-money against him) because the one present may take his right, and delaying until the other comes is not obligatory since his right is certain, while the right of the other is uncertain due to possibility of not claiming or pardoning. So it becomes like one of two co-preemptors when one is present and the other absent, where the present one is given pre-emption for the whole, as we said. Then if the other comes after it has been cut for the first, and claims, it is decreed for him the blood-money because his hand: fulfilling it is a right due from him, so he guarantees it for its soundness. And if retaliation was decreed between them and then one of them pardons before taking the blood-money, then the other has qawad according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and according to Muhammad he has arsh because retaliation by decree established partnership between them, so each one's right becomes partial; when one pardons, he prevents the other from taking all. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), that execution is in punishments, so pardon before it is like pardon before decree. And if one of them cuts the hand of the cutter from the elbow, the qisas falls due to the loss of the hand in which qisas was taken wrongfully, and he does not become owner, like if a stranger cuts it or it falls by a heavenly calamity. And both have half the blood-money as it was, because it was due before the cut and is not erased by the cut. Then the first cutter has the option: if he wishes, he cuts the forearm of the cutter, and if he wishes, he guarantees him the blood-money of the hand and the just value for cutting the forearm to the elbow because the cutter's hand was cut. The palm from when the first cutter cut from the elbow was like a paralyzed hand. And on this, if the victim's hand was one and the cutter cut from the elbow, his right in qisas falls and qisas is obligatory upon him, and the one whose hand was cut from the elbow has the option: if he wishes, he cuts from the elbow, and if he wishes, he takes the arsh, as we mentioned and previously provided further explanation.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn phrase 'وإن حضر واحد فقطع يده له فللآخر عليه نصف الدية'. The commentator explains that when two victims' hands are cut by one person, if only one victim is present and cuts the cutter's hand, he may do so because his right is certain. The absent victim's right is uncertain (might not claim or might forgive), so he only gets half diyah later. This is analogous to pre-emption (shuf'a) where one co-preemptor present takes all. Then additional scenarios: if the victims initially both got a decree of qisas, then one pardons before execution, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the other may still execute qisas (qawad), but Muhammad says only arsh remains. The preferred view is that pardon before execution is like pardon before decree, so qisas is not invalidated. Also, if one victim unjustly cuts the cutter's hand from the elbow before retaliation, both still get half diyah, and the first cutter may choose to cut the forearm or get compensation. This shows the nuanced rules of qisas when partial fulfillment occurs.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption; right of a co-owner to buy out the share of a new buyer. Here analogized for the right of the present victim.
قود (qawad): Execution of qisas; the physical act of retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario of two victims and one present, why does the absent victim only get half diyah?
A) Because the present victim's right is certain and the absent's uncertain.
B) Because the absent victim is not entitled to any compensation.
C) Because the absent victim must also appear to cut.
D) Because the cutter has only one hand.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qisas, a certain right to retaliation takes precedence over an uncertain one, analogized to pre-emption, and the absent co-victim receives only diyah.

Validity of a Slave's Confession to Intentional Murder
Ruling with proof on slave's confession to intentional murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a slave's confession to intentional murder and distinguish it from invalid confessions to property or manslaughter.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to intentional murder is valid because it harms himself, not his master; financial confessions harm the master and are void.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the Hanafi position on a slave's confession to intentional murder is clarified. The ruling turns on the principle that a confession against one's own interest is credible, while a confession that harms another (the master) is not. The passage contrasts three types of confessions to illustrate the logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a slave's confession (iqrar) to intentional murder (qatl amd). The Hanafi ruling is that the confession is valid and qisas is executed upon the slave.
Valid = sahih, leading to retributive execution.
2
Note the opposing view
Imam Zufr argued that such a confession is invalid because it leads to the invalidation of the master's right (the slave's value). He analogized it to confession to wrongful killing or property, which are invalid.
Zufr was an early scholar, but his view is rejected.
3
Locate the Hanafi proof (lana)
The Hanafi proof comprises three points: (1) the confession harms the slave himself, so he is not suspected of lying; (2) the slave retains original freedom in blood matters due to his humanity; (3) the master's own confession regarding hadd and qisas on the slave is invalid, so the slave's must be valid.
The proof is based on self-interest and analogy.
4
Distinguish analogous cases
Confession to property (mal) or manslaughter (khata) are invalid because their consequence falls directly on the master (sale of slave or ransom). The harm to the master is intentional, not incidental.
This contrast clarifies the criterion: who bears the harm.
5
Extract the underlying principle
A confession that harms the confessor is valid because it is against his interest. A confession that harms a third party is invalid because the confessor is suspected of collusion or carelessness.
This principle applies across many fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أقر عبد بقتل عمد يقتص منه وقال زفر رحمه الله : لا يصح إقراره لأنه يؤدي إلى إبطال المولى فصار كالإقرار بالقتل خطأ أو بالمال ولنا أنه غير متهم في مثله لكونه يلحقه الضرر به فيصح، ولأن العبد يبقى على أصل الحرية في حق الدم عملاً بآدميته ألا ترى أن إقرار المولى عليه بالحدود والقصاص لا يجوز فإذا صح لزمه إبطال حق المولى ضرورة وذلك لا يضر، وكم من شيء يصح ضمناً وإن كان لا يصح قصداً بخلاف الإقرار بالمال لأنه إقرار على المولى بإبطال قصداً لأن موجبه بيع العبد أو الاستيفاء، وكذا إقراره بالقتل خطأ لأن موجبه دفع العبد أو الفداء على المولى ولا يجب على العبد شيء ولا يصح سواء كان العبد محجوراً عليه أو مأذوناً له في التجارة لأنه باطل.
If a slave confesses to intentional murder, qisas is executed upon him. Zufr (may Allah have mercy on him) said: His confession is not valid because it leads to the master's right being invalidated, so it becomes like confession to wrongful killing or to property. Our evidence: He is not accused in such a matter because it brings harm upon him, so it is valid. And because the slave remains upon the original freedom (principle) with respect to blood, according to his humanity. Do you not see that the master's confession regarding hadd penalties and qisas is not valid? So if it (the slave's confession) is valid, it necessarily invalidates the master's right, and that is not harmful. How many things are valid implicitly though not valid explicitly? Unlike confession to property, because it is a confession against the master intending to invalidate his right, since its consequence is either the sale of the slave or execution (of the debt). Likewise, his confession to wrongful killing (manslaughter) because its consequence is the handing over of the slave or ransom upon the master, and nothing becomes obligatory on the slave. It is not valid whether the slave is interdicted from dealing or permitted to trade, because it is void.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the chapter on qisas. The passage addresses the validity of a slave's confession to intentional murder. The Hanafi position, contra Imam Zufr, is that the slave's confession is valid and leads to qisas on the slave. The reasoning: the confession is against the slave's own interest (it brings death upon him), so he is not suspected of lying. The slave retains the 'original freedom' in blood matters due to his humanity; thus, his confession regarding his life is valid even if it harms the master's property right (the slave's value). This is unlike confession to financial liability (debt or wrongful killing) which directly affects the master's property; such confessions are invalid because the slave cannot be trusted to admit something that harms the master without cause. The passage also notes that the master's confession regarding hudud or qisas on the slave is not valid. The conclusion: the slave's confession to intentional murder is valid and qisas is implemented; his confession to manslaughter or debt is invalid.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A confession or admission that establishes rights against the confessor.
عبد (abd): A male slave; in this context, a legal person with limited capacity but with full criminal liability for his own acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a Hanafi scholar consider a slave's confession to intentional murder valid despite the harm to the master?
A) Because the slave's testimony is always accepted in criminal cases.
B) Because the confession harms the slave himself, so he is not suspected of lying.
C) Because the master can object and invalidate the confession.
D) Because the confession is made in front of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to intentional murder is valid because it is against his own interest, unlike financial confessions which harm the master and are thus invalid.

Merger of Penalties in Cutting then Killing: The General Principle and the Exception
States the general rule and exception for liability when cutting precedes killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when cutting and killing merge into a single diyah and identify the conditions for the exception.

Core Idea
Two intentional acts never merge; two unintentional acts merge only if no healing intervened.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a scenario where a person cuts off another's hand and then kills him. The default rule is that both acts carry separate penalties. However, an exception applies when both acts are unintentional and the wound from the cut did not heal before death, resulting in a single diyah. Understanding this exception helps clarify the Hanafi doctrine of tadakhul (merger) and the role of sirayah (progression) in liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts
Recognize that cutting the hand and killing the person are two separate acts with potentially separate legal consequences.
The acts are distinct in time and type.
2
State the default ruling
The perpetrator is liable for the consequence of both acts (qisas or diyah) regardless of whether the acts are intentional, one intentional one unintentional, or both unintentional.
Default: no merger.
3
Identify the exception
Exception applies only when both acts are unintentional (khatā') and no healing (bur') occurs between them. In that case, only one diyah is due.
The condition 'no healing' is crucial.
4
Understand the rationale
The two acts are treated as a single harmful continuum without recovery. Healing would indicate the first wound was independent, thus requiring separate penalties.
Bur' blocks sirayah (progression).
5
Apply the condition
If healing does intervene, the acts remain separate even if both unintentional.
Example: victim's hand wound heals before death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن قطع يد رجل ثم قتله أخذ بالأمرين ولو عمدين أو مختلفين أو خطئين تخلل بينهما برء أو لا إلا في خطئين لم يتخلل بينهما برء فتجب دية واحدة كمن ضرب رجلاً مائة سوط فبرئ من تسعين ومات من عشرة يعني إذا قطع يده ثم قتله يجب عليه موجب القطع وموجب القتل إن كانا عمدين أو أحدهما عمد والآخر خطأ أو كانا خطئين وتخلل بينهما برء وفي خطئين لم يتخلل بينهما برء فتجب عليه دية واحدة، فحاصله أن الكل لا يتداخل إلا في خطئين فإنهما يتداخلان فيجب فيهما دية واحدة، إذا لم يتخلل بينهما برء، وإن تخلل بينهما برء لا يتداخلان.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Whoever cuts off a man's hand and then kills him, he is dealt with for both acts, even if both are intentional, or different, or both are unintentional with healing intervening or not, except in the case of two unintentional acts where no healing intervenes, then only one diyah is obligatory), like someone who strikes a man one hundred lashes, then he recovers from ninety and dies from ten. Meaning: if he cuts off his hand and then kills him, he is liable for the consequence of the cut and the consequence of the killing if both are intentional, or one is intentional and the other unintentional, or both are unintentional with healing intervening; and in the case of two unintentional acts where no healing intervenes, only one diyah is obligatory. So the summary is that [the two acts] do not merge except in two unintentional acts, for they merge and one diyah is obligatory, if no healing intervenes; but if healing intervenes, they do not merge.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq with its commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The ruling addresses a scenario where one person cuts off the hand of another and then kills him. The default rule is that both acts are taken into account: the perpetrator incurs the penalty for the cut (e.g., qisas in the hand if intentional, or diyah if unintentional) and the penalty for the killing (qisas or diyah). However, there is an exception: if both the cutting and the killing were unintentional (line) and there was no healing between them (i.e., the wound from the cut was still fresh when the death occurred), then the two acts merge, and only one diyah is due. This is because the death is attributed to the combined effect, and the act is treated as a single injury. The matn gives the example of a person who strikes someone one hundred lashes; the victim recovers from ninety lashes but dies from the remaining ten. Here, only one diyah is due because the lashes from which he recovered had no lasting effect; the death is from the ten. The commentator clarifies that the summary is that merging (tadakhul) occurs only in the case of two unintentional acts without healing; otherwise, each act has its own consequence.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Merging or overlapping of two legal consequences so that only one penalty applies instead of two.
برء (bur'): Healing of the wound, meaning the wound from the first act closes and no longer contributes to the death.
موجب القطع (mujib al-qat'): The legal consequence of cutting, which is either qisas (if intentional) or diyah (if unintentional).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In what specific case do the cutting and killing merge into one diyah?
A) When both acts are intentional.
B) When both acts are unintentional and no healing intervenes between them.
C) When one act is intentional and the other unintentional.
D) When healing intervenes between two unintentional acts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, two unintentional acts causing injury then death merge into one diyah only if no healing occurred between them.

Qisas — Retaliation

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Merging Intentional Cutting and Killing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on merging qisas for two intentional acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the merger of two intentional qisas acts and explain the legal reasoning (dalil) behind each.

Core Idea
Cutting the throat prevents progression, so two intentional acts remain separate for qisas.

When a perpetrator first cuts off someone's hand and then immediately kills the same victim, should the two intentional acts be punished separately or merged? Abu Hanifa insists on separate qisas for each, while his two foremost disciples disagree. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and clarifies the practical implications for a guardian seeking justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Note that Abu Hanifa holds the acts do not merge (separate qisas for cutting and killing), whereas the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold they merge (only one penalty – killing).
2
Examine the Sahibayn's argument
They argue merger is possible due to similarity of acts (both bodily harms) and no healing between them; they analogize to unintentional acts where wounds combine. The principle is combining to avoid hardship.
Also note the exception: merger is not possible if acts differ in ruling (e.g., one intentional, one unintentional) or if healing intervenes.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
He argues merging is impossible because cutting the throat (in killing) prevents progression (sirayah) of the hand wound, just like healing. Thus the acts are independent. Qisas requires exact equivalence in form and meaning; killing alone does not equal cutting.
He further distinguishes from cases of progression (sirayah) where only one act exists, and from unintentional cases where diyah merges because it compensates the same victim.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa: the perpetrator's hand is cut off first, then he is killed (guardian may choose to forgo the cut). Under the Sahibayn: he is simply killed, no hand amputation.
The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) presents Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.

Source Text

أما الأول وهو ما إذا كانا عمدين فالمذكور قول أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يتداخلان فيقتل حداً ولا يقطع يده لأن الجمع بينهما ممكن لتجانس الفعلين وعدم تخلل البرء بينهما فصار كالخطاين، وهذا لأن الجمع بين الجراحات واجب ما أمكن لأن القتل يقع بضربات غالباً واعتبار كل ضربة على حدتها يؤدي إلى الحرج فيجمع تيسيراً إلا أن لا يمكن بأن يختلف حكم الفعلين كالعمد والخطأ أو يتخلل البرء بينهما لأن البرء قاطع للسراية فلا يمكن أن يجعل الثاني تتميماً للأول فيعتبر على حاله وأمكن ذلك قبل البرء فصار كسراية الأول. وله أن الجمع متعذر لأن حز الرقبة يمنع سراية القطع كالبرء حتى لو صدر من شخصين وجب على كل واحد منهما القصاص فكذا إذا كان من شخص واحد فتقطع أولاً يده ثم يقتلوه إن شاؤوا وإن شاؤوا قتلوه من غير قطع لأن القصاص يعتمد المساواة في الفعل وذلك بأن يكون القتل بالقتل والقطع بالقطع واستيفاء القطع بالقتل متعذر لاختلافهما حقيقة وحكماً، ولأن المماثلة صورة ومعنى يكون باستيفائهما وبالاكتفاء بالقتل لم توجد المماثلة إلا معنى فلا يصار إليه مع القدرة على المماثلة صورة ومعنى فيخير الولي بخلاف ما إذا مات من السراية لأن الفعل واحد وبخلاف ما إذا كان خطأين لأن الموجب فيه الدية وهو بدل المحل والمقتول واحد ألا ترى أن عشرة لو قتلوا واحداً خطأ يجب عليهم دية واحدة لاتحاد المحل وإن تعدد الفعل، ولو قتلوه به جميعاً لأن القصاص جزاء الفعل وهو متعدد وإن اتحد، ولأن أرش اليد لو وجب كان يجب عليه عند الجزاء لأنه وقت استحكام أثر الفعل ولا سبيل إليه لأنه حينئذ تجب دية النفس بالجزاء فيجتمع وجوب بدل الجزاء والكل في حالة واحدة وهو محال، ولو وجب ذلك لوجب بقتل النفس الواحد ديات كثيرة للأطراف لأنها تتلف بتلف النفس، أما القتل والقطع فقصاصان فأمكن اجتماعهما، وبخلاف ما إذا قطع وسرى حيث يكتفي بالقطع لاتحاد الفعل.
As for the first case, when both are intentional: the stated position is that of Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they merge, so he is killed by execution only and his hand is not cut off, because combining [the two penalties] is possible due to the similarity of the two acts and the absence of healing intervening, so it becomes like two unintentional acts. This is because combining wounds is obligatory as much as possible, since killing usually occurs through multiple blows, and considering each blow separately leads to hardship, so they are combined for ease, unless it is not possible, such as when the rulings of the two acts differ (like intentional and unintentional) or when healing intervenes, because healing breaks the progression (sirayah), so the second cannot be made a completion of the first, so each is considered on its own; but it was possible before healing, so it becomes like the progression of the first. And for Abu Hanifa: combining is impossible because cutting the throat prevents progression of the cut, like healing. So even if two persons did it, each would be liable for qisas; likewise if one person did it: his hand is cut off first, then he is killed if the guardians wish, or they may kill him without cutting because qisas is based on equivalence in the act: killing is by killing, cutting by cutting. It is impossible to fulfill the cutting by killing because they differ in reality and ruling. And because equivalence in form and meaning is achieved by fulfilling both; if only killing is done, equivalence is only in meaning, so it is not resorted to when equivalence in both form and meaning is possible. So the guardian has a choice. Unlike when death occurs by progression (sirayah), because the act is one. And unlike when both are unintentional, because the obligation is diyah, which is compensation for the person, and the victim is one; do you not see that if ten persons unintentionally kill one person, only one diyah is upon them because the subject is one, even if the acts are multiple? And if they all kill him together [intentionally], qisas is on each because qisas is the recompense for the act, which is multiple even if the victim is one. And because if the compensation (arsh) for the hand were due, it would be due at the time of recompense, because that is when the effect of the act is established, but this is impossible because at that moment the diyah for the soul is due by recompense, so both would be due simultaneously, which is impossible. If that were the case, for killing one soul there would be multiple diyats for limbs because the limbs are destroyed with the soul. In contrast, cutting and killing are both qisas, so they can coexist. And unlike when he cuts and it progresses, in which case only the cut is sufficient because the act is one.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the case where both the cutting of the hand and the subsequent killing are intentional. Abu Hanifa holds that the two acts do not merge: the perpetrator's hand must be cut off as qisas for the cutting, and then he must be killed as qisas for the killing. The guardian (wali) has the option to demand both or to forgive the cutting and only demand the killing. His reasoning is that qisas requires exact equivalence in form and meaning; killing the perpetrator does not fulfill the cut because the cut is a specific amputation. Additionally, cutting the throat (in killing) prevents the progression of the earlier wound, so the two are separate. The Sahibayn hold that the two acts merge into a single qatl (killing): the perpetrator is simply killed, and the hand is not cut off. They argue that combining the two penalties is possible because the acts are similar (both are violations of the body) and there is no healing between them, so it is like two unintentional acts where merger occurs. They analogize to the principle that wounds are combined as much as possible to avoid hardship. However, Abu Hanifa responds that the degree of hardship is outweighed by the necessity of exact equivalence in qisas. The passage also clarifies that this case differs from death by progression (sirayah) where only one act is involved, and from unintentional acts where one diyah suffices because compensation is for the same victim.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): In this context, merging of two qisas penalties so that only one is applied.
سراية (sirayah): Progression of a wound leading to death; cuts off by healing or by a separate fatal act like throat cutting.
مماثلة صورة ومعنى (mumathalah suratan wa ma'nan): Equivalence in both form (physical act) and meaning (legal effect) - a principle in qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the acts are of different types (one intentional, one unintentional).
D) Because the guardian prefers to have both penalties.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa holds that intentional acts of cutting and killing do not merge in qisas because throat-cutting prevents progression, requiring separate penalties for each act.

Two Accidental Injuries Without Recovery: One Diyah
Expansion and proof of the overlapping injuries rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on multiple accidental injuries without recovery, identify the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, and apply the principle to distinguish between overlapping and separate injuries.

Core Idea
When two accidental injuries occur without recovery, they merge: only the diyah for death is due.

In Hanafi law, the concept of recovery (barā') is critical for separating injuries. If a victim recovers between two injuries, each injury is treated independently. But if no recovery intervenes, the injuries are considered a single continuous act, and only the most severe outcome—death—triggers a single diyah. This lesson unpacks the rule, its intra-Hanafi variations, and a cross-madhhab dispute on sequential cutting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text addresses two accidental injuries without recovery leading to death. The ruling: one diyah is due, no arsh for the first injury.
This is an exception to the general rule that each harmful act has its own obligation.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author uses an example: striking a man with 100 lashes (he recovers), then 90 lashes causing death. The proof is that when the first injury leaves no trace, its compensation drops.
The proof is based on reasoning (qiyas) and the principle of removal of disfigurement.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative link: if the trace (shin) is gone, the arsh for the first injury is no longer justified, leaving only the diyah for death.
If a trace remains, both arsh and diyah are due by consensus.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds only diyah; Abu Yusuf adds hukumat adl; Muhammad adds medical expenses.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
For sequential cutting by two persons, Hanafi says only the second cutter is liable for murder; Zufar and Shafi'i say both are executed.
The Hanafi response distinguishes that only the second cut's pain caused death, as the first cut's site is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات باب القصاص فيما دون النفس غير ممكن فيهما لاختلاف حكم الفعلين في الأول ولتخلل البرء في الثاني وهو قاطع للسراية فيعطي لكل فعل حكم نفسه. وقوله «لا في خطأين لم يتخلل بينهما برء فتجب دية واحدة وهذا إخراج من قوله آخذ بالأمرين أي موجبي فعله إلا في هذه الصورة فإنهما يتداخلان لا يؤخذ إلا بالقتل فيجب فيه دية النفس لا غير وقد بينا وجهه في أثناء البحث. وقوله «كمن ضرب رجلاً مائة سوط فبرئ ومن تسعين ومات من عشرة» يعني تجب فيه دية واحدة كما إذا كان القطع والقتل خطأين ولم يتخلل بينهما برء وإنما كان كذلك لأن الضربات التي برأ منها ولم يبق لها أثر سقط أرشها لزوال الشين، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وعن أبي يوسف وفيها حكومة عدل وعن محمد أنه يجب فيها أجرة الطبيب وثمن الأدوية وستأتي المسألة بأدلتها في فصل الشجاج إن شاء الله تعالى. ولو بقي لها أثر بعد البرء يجب موجبه مع دية النفس بالإجماع لأن الأرش يجب باعتبار الشين في النفس وهو ببقاء الأثر، ولو قطع أصبعه أو يده ثم قطع الآخر ما بقي من اليد فمات كان القصاص على الثاني في النفس دون الأول ويقطع أصابع الأول أو يده. وقال زفر والشافعي : يقتلان لهما أن زوال الحياة مضاف إلى القطعين لأنه اتصل الموت بهما قبل البرء وزال أثرهما وليس أحدهما بإضافة الإزهاق إليه أولى من الآخر فأضيف إليهما كما لو قطع كل واحد منهما يداً على حدة قبل البرء ولنا أن زوال الحياة ألم الثاني غير قطع الأول فصار زوال الحياة مضافاً إلى القطع الثاني فصار الثاني قتلا دون الأول بخلاف ما لو قطع كل واحد يداً على حدة أو أصبعاً على حدة لأن محل قطع الأول قائم وقت الموت فيتصور منه حدوث زيادة الألم فحصل بألم حدث القطعين فصار الموت مضافاً إليهما.
The Book of Crimes, Chapter of Retaliation for Less than Homicide. Not possible in them due to difference in the ruling of the two acts in the first and due to recovery intervening in the second, which cuts off continuity, so each act is given its own ruling. And His statement: 'No, in two accidental injuries with no recovery between them, so one blood money is obligatory.' And this is an exception to his statement: 'I take both obligations' meaning the two requisites of the act, except in this case they overlap; only death is taken, so the blood money of the life is obligatory, nothing else. And we have explained its aspect in the course of the research. And His statement: 'Like one who strikes a man one hundred lashes and he recovers, then ninety and he dies from ten' means that one blood money is obligatory therein, just as if the cutting and killing were both accidental and no recovery intervened between them. And it is so because the strikes from which he recovered and which left no trace, their compensation is dropped due to the removal of the disfigurement. This is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And from Abu Yusuf: in them is a just assessment (hukumat adl). And from Muhammad: in them is owed the doctor's fee and cost of medicines. And the issue will come with its proofs in the chapter of head wounds, if Allah wills. And if a trace remains after recovery, its due is obligatory along with the blood money of the life by consensus, because compensation is obligatory in consideration of the disfigurement in the life, and it is by the persistence of the trace. And if he cut his finger or hand, then another cut what remained of the hand and he died, then the retaliation is upon the second in life, not the first, and the fingers of the first or his hand are cut. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: Both are killed, because removal of life is attributed to both cuts since death followed them before recovery and their effect ceased, and it is not that attribution of killing to one is more appropriate than the other, so it is attributed to both just as if each of them cut a hand separately before recovery. And our proof: that removal of life is by the pain of the second, not by the cut of the first, so removal of life is attributed to the second cut, so the second becomes a killing without the first, contrary to if each cut a hand separately or a finger separately, because the site of the first cut is present at the time of death, so occurrence of additional pain is conceivable, so it occurs by the pain of the occurrence of both cuts, so death becomes attributed to both.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rule regarding multiple injuries and the effect of recovery. The commentator explains that two acts cannot both be subject to retaliation when their legal effects differ (e.g. one is cutting the other is killing) or when recovery intervenes between them, cutting off the chain of causation. The matn statement 'لا في خطأين لم يتخلل بينهما برء فتجب دية واحدة' is an exception to the general principle that both obligations apply; here, because both acts are accidental and no recovery occurs, they overlap and only one diyah of the life is due. The example of lashes illustrates: 100 lashes followed by recovery, then 90 lashes causing death. According to Abu Hanifa, since the first set left no trace, their compensation drops, and only one diyah for death applies. Abu Yusuf holds there is a just assessment for the first set, and Muhammad says the medical expenses are due. The text then notes that if a trace remains after recovery, both the arsh (compensation for the injury) and the diyah for life are due by consensus. Finally, the case of sequential cutting by two persons leading to death is discussed: the Hanafi position holds that only the second cutter is subject to qisas for life, because the death is attributed to the pain caused by the second cut, not the first. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue both are liable, comparing it to two simultaneous cuts. The Hanafi refutation distinguishes that when the first site is still present, it can contribute additional pain, but here the first cut's site is removed, so only the second causes death.

Key Terms
برء (barā'): Recovery from an injury, which cuts off the chain of causation between the injury and death.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a body part, distinct from diyah (blood money for the whole person).
دية (diyah): Blood money paid for causing death or loss of a body part in certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Arsh for first set and diyah for second
D) No liability because he died from a combination

Key Takeaway
Two accidental injuries without recovery merge into a single obligation: only the diyah for death is due, provided the first injury leaves no trace.

Sequential Cuts and Recovery: Qisas Only for the First Joint
Ruling on qisas for sequential cuts of joints on the same finger with recovery intervening

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of musawah (equality) to determine when qisas is due and when arsh is substituted in cases of sequential bodily injuries.

Core Idea
Qisas requires exact equality at the moment of execution; recovery breaks continuity, so later cuts are compensated by arsh.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario in the law of qisas: when a victim suffers sequential cuts to the same finger, with healing (barā') in between. The Hanafi school holds that the condition of musawah (equality) must be satisfied at the time of each cut. Since the first cut eliminated the victim's upper joint, the cutter's finger remains intact at the time of the second cut — equality is impossible, and thus arsh (compensation) replaces qisas for the subsequent joints. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is cutting the upper joint, then healing, then cutting the lower joint of the same finger. The ruling: qisas for the first, arsh for the second.
The ruling depends on the presence of recovery between cuts.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is qiyas (analogy) based on the principle that qisas requires equality (musawah). The text provides a detailed illa (effective cause): recovery (barā') prevents treating the cuts as a single injury.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted by tahrir al-manat.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
At the time of the second cut, the cutter's finger is sound while the victim's joint is already missing. Equality is impossible, so qisas cannot be exacted; arsh is substituted.
The condition of musawah is evaluated at the time of each cut, not at the time of the first injury.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text implies that other schools may allow qisas for all cuts. The Hanafi position is rooted in strict equality.
Cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the thamarah (outcome)
Practical consequence: victim gets qisas only for the first joint; for the later joint, he receives arsh. If no recovery intervenes, all cuts are treated as one injury and qisas is due for all joints at once.
The ruling changes based on the presence or absence of recovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قطع المفصل الأعلى من أصبع رجل فبرئ ولم يقتص حتى قطع مفصلاً آخر من تلك الأصبع يقطع له المفصل الأعلى دون الأسفل وعليه أرش من الأسفل لأن القصاص مبناه على المساواة وحال قطع الثاني لا يمكن المساواة لسلامة أصبع القاطع وفوات مفصل المقطوع، ولأن أصبع القاطع وإن كانت مستحقة بالقصاص ولكن ملك القصاص ملك ضرورة لا يثبت إلا عند الاستيفاء فقتله يكون مقصوداً به مملوكية صاحبه، ولهذا لو قلنا لو قطعت يد من عليه القصاص إن كان عمداً يجب القصاص، وإن كان خطأ يجب الأرش له لا لمن له القصاص لأنه لم توجد المساواة حال قطع الثاني، وكذلك لو أبرأ الثاني ثم قطع المفصل الثالث ولو لم يكن القطعين برئ ووجب له القصاص في كل الأصابع. بقطعها من أصلها مرة واحدة لأنه لم يتخلل بين القطعين برء وجعلنا كلا الفعلين جناية واحدة كأنه قطع ابتداء من المفصل الثاني بفعل واحد.
And if he cuts the upper joint of a man's finger, and he recovers, and he does not exact retaliation until he cuts another joint of that finger, then the upper joint is cut for him (the victim) but not the lower, and upon him is the arsh for the lower because retaliation is built upon equality, and at the time of the second cut equality is not possible due to the soundness of the cutter's finger and the loss of the victim's joint, and because the cutter's finger, even if it is subject to retaliation, yet the ownership of retaliation is a necessity that is only established at the time of execution, so his killing is intended for the ownership of its owner. For this reason, if we say, if the hand of the person upon whom is retaliation is cut, if intentional, retaliation is required; if accidental, the arsh is for him, not for the one who has the right to retaliation, because equality does not exist at the time of the second cut. Likewise, if the second joint heals, then the third joint is cut. And if the two cuts had not healed, then he is entitled to retaliation in all the fingers, cutting them from their base all at once because no recovery intervened between the two cuts, and we consider both acts as one single injury, as if he cut from the second joint with one act.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage discusses the specific case of sequential cuts to the same finger with recovery in between. The ruling is that the victim can only demand qisas for the first joint cut, not for subsequent joints, because the cutter's finger remains intact (since he has not yet been punished), and thus equality (musawah) cannot be achieved for the later joints. Instead, the victim is entitled to arsh (compensation) for the lower joints. The reasoning is that the right to qisas is a right of ownership that is only established at the moment of execution; if the cutter's finger is still intact, the victim cannot cut it in retaliation because it is not equal to the already cut joint. The text also considers cases of healing and non-healing, and compares to the earlier principle that if no recovery intervenes, the acts are treated as one injury and qisas can be taken for all joints at once.

Key Terms
المفصل (mufassal): A joint of a finger; the text refers to the upper (a'lā) and lower (asfal) joints.
مساواة (musāwāh): Equality between the injury inflicted and the retaliation exacted; a condition for qisas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, qisas requires exact equality at the time of each injury; healing breaks the chain, converting later cuts to arsh.

Qisas — Retaliation

When Qisas Is Precluded Due to the Killer: Conversion to Diyah
Principle of conversion to diyah when impediment from killer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that when the impossibility of executing qisas arises from the killer's side, the penalty converts to diyah, and distinguish this from Zufar's qiyas-based position.

Core Idea
When the killer's own action prevents qisas, diyah becomes due to protect the victim's blood.

This passage from al-Mabsut establishes a key principle in Hanafi criminal law: if qisas cannot be carried out because of an impediment caused by the killer—such as claiming the killing was accidental—then the penalty converts to diyah. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to ensure the victim's right to compensation is not lost. Zufar, a prominent Hanafi scholar, disagreed, arguing by qiyas that no liability should arise. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling, the proof, and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Impediment
The killer confesses to accidental killing, but the guardian (wali) claims intentional. Both agree on the killing but differ on intent.
The impediment to qisas is the killer's own claim of accident.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
By istihsan, diyah (blood money) is wajib (obligatory) on the killer.
The ruling is based on the principle that if the impediment comes from the killer, the penalty converts to wealth.
3
Analyze Zufar's Qiyas
Zufar argues by qiyas that if the killer confesses to accidental but the guardian claims intentional, the confession is not established because the guardian's claim contradicts it; thus nothing is due.
Zufar compares to the case where the killer confesses to intentional and guardian claims accidental—then nothing is due.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response: both parties agree on the killing; the impediment arises from the killer's side (the claim of accident); therefore, diyah is due to avoid wastage of blood. The killer's confession to accident is essentially a confession to the financial obligation.
The guardian may forgo qisas and take the diyah.

وفي المبسوط : أصله أن تعذر استيفاء القصاص لتعذر القتل أنه متى جاء من قبل القاتل فصار إلى المال اعتباراً بالخطأ فإن هناك امتنع استيفاء القصاص بمعنى من جهة القاتل عن القطع وما يحدث منه أو عن الجناية لا فالخطأ من الثلث والعمد من كل المال وإن وهو الخطأ، فإذا تعذر صيانة الاستيفاء القصاص من قبل من له الحق لا يصار إلى المال لأن الشرع غير حقه في القصاص لكن هو الذي فوته وفرط بإتيان ما أعجزه فأهدره فلم يبق مستحقاً للنظر، وإذا أقر القاتل بالخطأ وادعى الولي العمد لم يقتص ولزمه الدية استحساناً. وقال زفر : لا يلزمه شيء قياساً لأن ما أقربه لم يثبت لأنه كذبه المدعي في إقراره بمقتضي دعواه القصاص وصار كما لو أقر القاتل بالعمد وادعى الولي الخطأ لا يلزمه شيء . هذا. ولنا أنهما تصادقا على القتل إلا أنه تعذر استيفاء القصاص بمعنى من قبل القاتل وهو دعوى الخطأ فتجب الدية صوناً لدمه عن الهدر ولأن في زعم الولي أن القصاص هو الواجب إلا إنه لما أقر بالخطأ فقد أقر بالمال وللولي ترك القصاص وأخذ المال ولم يكن به صريحاً فيكون له أخذ المال. ولو أقر بالعمد وادعى الولي الخطأ بطل حقه لا تعذر استيفاء القصاص جاء من قبل من له الحق الزيادات ولو ادعى الولي العمد على رجلين فقال أحدهما أنا قطعت يده عمداً وهذا الآخر قطع رجله عمداً وأنكر الآخر الجناية قال: يقتص من المقر لأنهما تصادقا على وجوب القود ولو لم تتمكن الشبهة فيه حين أنكر الآخر الجناية لأنه يمكن الشبهة إنما يكون باختلاط الموجب وغير الموجب في المحل وذلك لا يتصور قبل وجوب الجناية من الآخر. وإذا ادعى الولي الخطأ فلا شيء على المقر لأنه لما أنكر الآخر الجناية صار كالعدم فبطل دعواه الخطأ، وإقرار القاتل بالعمد في هذا لا يجب شيء. وإن مات رجل من قطع يده ورجله فقال رجل قطعت يده عمداً وقال قطع عمرو رجله عمداً فقال الولي بل أنت قطعتهما يجب القصاص عليه لأنهما تصادقا عل وجوب القصاص والشركة لم تثبت لعدم دعواه، فإن قال الولي لا أدري من قطع رجله فلا شيء على قاطع اليد لأن قاطع الرجل مجهول يجز أن يكون خاطئاً أو صبياً أو مجنوناً فتعذر إيجاب القصاص، وتعذر استيفاء القصاص جاء من قبل من له الحق فإن جهل قاطع الرجل جهل قاطع اليد فلا يجب المال. ولو قال الولي بعد ذلك فلان قطع رجله عمداً وأنكر فلان ليس له أن يقتل المقر قياساً وله أن يقتله استحساناً لأن الولد لا يعرف قاتل أبيه عند كثرتهم فيعتذر في التناقض .
And in al-Mabsut: Its principle is that if the execution of qisas becomes impossible due to the impossibility of killing, when it comes from the side of the killer, it turns to wealth by analogy with accidental killing, for there the execution of qisas is prevented by a meaning from the side of the killer. Regarding the cutting and what results from it or regarding the injury, no, so the accidental is from one-third and the intentional from all the wealth. And it is accidental. So when the execution of qisas is prevented from the side of the one who has the right, it does not turn to wealth because the law changed his right to qisas, but he is the one who wasted and neglected by doing what incapacitated it, so he nullified it, so he remains not deserving of consideration. And if the killer confesses to accidental and the guardian claims intentional, he is not subject to qisas and the diyah is incumbent upon him by istihsan. Zufar said: Nothing is incumbent upon him by qiyas because what he confessed to is not established since the claimant contradicts his confession with his claim of qisas, and it becomes like if the killer confesses to intentional and the guardian claims accidental, nothing is incumbent upon him. Our proof: They both agree on the killing, but execution of qisas is prevented by a meaning from the side of the killer, namely his claim of accidental, so the diyah becomes obligatory to protect his blood from going to waste. And because in the guardian's view, qisas is obligatory, but since he confessed to accidental, he has confessed to wealth, and the guardian may forgo qisas and take wealth, and he was not explicit about it, so he may take the wealth. And if he confesses to intentional and the guardian claims accidental, his right is void because the prevention of qisas came from the side of the one who has the right, the additions. And if the guardian claims intentional against two men, and one says: I cut his hand intentionally and this other cut his leg intentionally, and the other denies the injury, he said: The one who confesses is subject to qisas because they both agree on the obligation of qisas, even if doubt does not occur when the other denies the injury, because doubt can only occur by mixing the cause and non-cause in the same locus, and that is not conceivable before the obligation of the injury from the other. And if the guardian claims accidental, then nothing is upon the one who confesses because when the other denies the injury, it becomes as if non-existent, so his claim of accidental fails. And the confession of the killer to intentional in this does not require anything. And if a man dies from the cutting of his hand and leg, and a man says: I cut his hand intentionally, and Amr cut his leg intentionally, and the guardian says: Rather you cut both, then qisas is upon him because they both agreed on the obligation of qisas, and partnership is not established due to lack of claim. If the guardian says: I do not know who cut his leg, then nothing is upon the cutter of the hand because the cutter of the leg is unknown, he could be mistaken or a child or insane, so the obligation of qisas becomes impossible, and the prevention of execution of qisas came from the side of the one who has the right. So if the cutter of the leg is unknown, the cutter of the hand is also unknown, so wealth is not obligatory. And if the guardian later says: So-and-so cut his leg intentionally, and so-and-so denies, he does not have the right to kill the one who confessed by qiyas, but he has the right to kill him by istihsan because the child does not know the killer of his father when many are present, and he is excused in the contradiction.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage from al-Mabsut lays down an important principle: when qisas cannot be executed due to a cause coming from the killer's side (e.g., he claims the killing was accidental, making the victim's family unsure), then the penalty converts to diyah. This is by istihsan (preference) to avoid leaving the blood of the victim without compensation. Zufar (a Hanafi dissenter) argues by qiyas that confession of accidental does not establish liability because the guardian's claim of intentional contradicts it. The Hanafi response is that both parties agree on the fact of killing; only the nature differs. Since the killer's claim of accidental prevents qisas, he must pay diyah. The passage then discusses various scenarios of confessions and denials, emphasizing that if the impossibility of qisas comes from the guardian's side (e.g., he does not know the killer), then no diyah is due. The text also discusses the case of two accused, one confesses and the other denies, and the case of multiple cuts by different persons. The theme is consistency: the burden of doubt falls on the party that creates the impediment.

Key Terms
تعذر استيفاء القصاص (ta'adhur istifa' al-qisas): Impossibility of executing qisas due to legal impediment.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to avoid a harsh qiyas outcome; here it leads to diyah instead of no liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a killer confesses to accidental killing, but the guardian claims it was intentional, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No liability because of contradiction
B) Qisas is executed based on the killer's confession
C) Diyah is due by istihsan
D) The killer is imprisoned until he changes his confession

Key Takeaway
When the killer causes the impediment to qisas, diyah becomes wajib by istihsan to avoid leaving the victim's blood uncompensated.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
6 min
Effect of Victim's Forgiveness on Diyah
Matn and commentary on forgiveness of cutting before death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of the victim's forgiveness of a cutting on liability for diyah when death subsequently occurs, and distinguish between forgiveness of the act versus forgiveness of the entire injury.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of the cut does not waive diyah for resultant death; only forgiveness of the entire injury limits the heirs' claim.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in qisas: when a victim forgives the act of cutting before dying, the cutter remains liable for diyah. However, if the victim forgives the cutting and its consequences (the entire injury), the heirs' claim to diyah is limited: the accidental case is paid from one-third of the cutter's estate, while the intentional case requires full diyah. Understanding the precise wording of forgiveness is key.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act forgiven
Determine exactly what the victim said: did he forgive only the cutting, or the entire injury and its consequences?
The wording matters greatly.
2
Note whether death resulted
The ruling applies only if the victim dies from the injury after the forgiveness.
3
Apply the ruling for forgiveness of the cut alone
If the victim said 'I forgive the cutting' and then dies, the cutter is liable for the full diyah (for intentional) or diyah from one-third (for accidental).
This holds even though the victim forgave the immediate act.
4
Apply the ruling for forgiveness of the entire injury
If the victim said 'I forgive the cutting and what may result' or 'I forgive the injury', then the diyah is limited: for accidental, from one-third of the cutter's estate; for intentional, from the entire wealth.
The victim's forgiveness of the entire injury is treated like a gift that takes effect only from one-third if accidental, but for intentional the full amount is due because the victim's right to diyah is not fully waived.
5
Understand the legal basis
Forgiveness of the cutting does not imply forgiveness of the subsequent death; the right to diyah for death is separate.
This prevents the cutter from evading liability through a narrow reading of the victim's words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبر المؤلف بـ«من» التي لفظها مفرد ومعناه جمع لأنه لا فرق في الحكم بين ما إذا كان الفاعل مفرداً أو متعدداً. قال رحمه الله : (فإن عفا المقطوع عن القطع فمات ضمن القاطع الدية ولو عفا عن القطع وما يحدث منه أو عن الجناية لا فالخطأ من الثلث والعمد من كل المال) يعني لو قطع يد رجل عمداً أو أخطأ فقال المقطوع عفوت عن القطع فمات ضمن القاطع في العمد الدية بخلاف ما لو قال عفوت عن الجناية كما سيأتي. وأطلق المؤلف في قوله والخطأ من ثلث المال ولم يفرق بين ما إذا كان العافي يخرج ويجيء أو كان لا يخرج سيأتي بيانه.
And the author used 'من' which is singular in form but plural in meaning because there is no difference in the ruling whether the doer is singular or plural. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If the victim forgives the cutting, then dies, the cutter is liable for the diyah; but if he forgives the cutting and what results from it, or forgives the injury, then no, so the accidental is from one-third of the estate and the intentional from all the wealth.' Meaning: if he cuts a man's hand intentionally or mistakenly, and the victim says: I forgive the cutting, then he dies, the cutter is liable for diyah in the case of intentionality, unlike if he said: I forgive the injury, as will come. And the author generalised in his saying: 'and the accidental is from one-third of the estate' and did not distinguish between whether the one who forgives goes out and comes in or not; its explanation will come.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage contains the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary. The author first notes that the word 'man' (من) is used generically for singular or plural doers. The matn states: if the victim forgives the act of cutting (but not its consequences), and then dies from the injury, the cutter must pay diyah. However, if the victim forgives both the cutting and its possible consequences (i.e., he forgives the injury completely), then the ruling differs: if the cutting was accidental, the diyah is only from one-third of the cutter's estate (meaning if he admits to accidental, the victim's heirs can only claim one-third of his estate? Actually, the implication is that the forgiveness of the injury itself waives the right to qisas, but if the victim later dies, the heirs' claim to diyah is limited: for accidental, it is one-third of the cutter's estate because the victim's forgiveness is considered a gift, and gifts are enforceable only up to one-third of the estate. For intentional, the full diyah is from the cutter's entire wealth because the victim cannot waive what he does not own (the right to qisas is personal, but forgiveness of intentional murder without compensation is not valid?) The commentary clarifies by example: if the cutting was intentional and the victim forgave the cutting, the diyah is still due; if he forgave the injury itself, the situation is different. The author notes that the matn did not specify whether the victim is still alive at the time of forgiveness or not; that detail will be explained later.

Key Terms
عفا (afā): Forgiveness by the victim of a crime before death, which can affect the liability of the perpetrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the victim says 'I forgive the cutting' and then dies from the injury, what is the ruling?
A) No liability because the victim forgave
B) The cutter is liable for diyah
C) The cutter is liable for qisas
D) The heirs must also forgive

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness of the cutting alone does not cancel diyah for the resulting death; only forgiveness of the entire injury can limit the heirs' claim to one-third for accidental or full for intentional.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

Qisas After Death: Heirs May Kill the Other Assailant
Ruling for qisas when death occurs after decree against one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two men wound intentionally, qisas is decreed against one, and the victim then dies from both wounds.

Core Idea
If death results from combined wounds, qisas can transfer to the other assailant.

This ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani addresses a nuanced situation in qisas. Two men each inflict an intentional wound. The qadi orders qisas against one, but before it is carried out, the victim dies from the cumulative effect of both wounds. Can the heirs now take qisas from the other? Muhammad's answer clarifies that liability is not extinguished by the initial decree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
Two men each intentionally wound the same victim. This is a case of joint intentional injury.
Both wounds are 'amdan (intentional).
2
Note the initial judicial decree
The qadi decrees qisas (retaliation) against one of them. The implication is that at that time, death had not yet occurred.
The decree is for the wound itself, not for murder.
3
Recognize the change in circumstance
The victim dies from the combined effect of both wounds. The death transforms the legal situation from wound to murder.
4
Apply the ruling
Muhammad says the heirs may kill the other assailant. This is because both are now liable for murder, and qisas against one does not preclude qisas against the other when the death results from both actions.
The initial decree does not exhaust the right of qisas.
5
Consider the basis of the ruling
The Hanafi principle: when multiple assailants contribute to death, each is liable for the full qisas unless the death is solely attributable to one. Here, death is from combined wounds, so both are liable.
This is from Nawadir (Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن سماعة عن محمد في رجل جرحه رجلان جراحة عمداً فقضى بالقصاص على أحدهما ثم مات من الجراحتين قال : لورثته أن يقتلوا الآخر، ولو جرحه رجل
Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: regarding a man who was wounded intentionally by two men, and qisas was decreed against one of them, then he died from the two wounds, he said: his heirs may kill the other.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a specific fatwa from Muhammad al-Shaybani via Ibn Sama'a. The case is: two men both intentionally wound the same victim. The qadi decrees qisas against one of them. However, before the qisas is executed, the victim dies from the combined effects of both wounds. The question is whether the heirs can now execute qisas against the other assailant (the one not originally decreed against). Muhammad's answer is yes, the heirs may kill the other. The reasoning is that since death resulted from both wounds, both are liable for murder. The initial decree against one does not preclude liability of the other after death. This shows that qisas can be transferred to the other participant when the death occurs after the decree. The text cuts off before completing the next case.

Key Terms
قتل الآخر (qatl al-akhar): The heirs may execute qisas against the other assailant who was not initially decreed against.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the ruling in this passage, if two men intentionally wound a man, qisas is decreed against one, then the victim dies from both wounds, what is the ruling?
A) The heirs can only execute qisas against the one originally decreed.
B) The heirs cannot execute qisas against anyone because the death was from combined wounds.
C) The heirs may kill the other assailant (the one not originally decreed).
D) The heirs must accept diya from both.

Key Takeaway
When death results from combined intentional wounds, a prior qisas decree for wound does not bar qisas for murder against the other participant.
